			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Renewable energy in the United States</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p><b>Renewable energy in the United States</b> accounted for 13.44 percent of the domestically produced electricity in 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and 11.1 percent of total energy generation.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> As of 2014, more than 143,000 people work in the solar industry and 43 states deploy <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a>, where energy utilities buy back excess power generated by <a href="/wiki/Solar_array" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar array">solar arrays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a> reached a major milestone in the first quarter of 2011, when it contributed 11.7 percent of total U.S. energy production (2.245 quadrillion BTU of energy), surpassing energy production from nuclear power (2.125 quadrillion BTU).<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> 2011 was the first year since 1997 that renewables exceeded nuclear in US total energy production.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroelectric power">Hydroelectric power</a> is currently the largest producer of renewable power in the U.S. It produced around 6.14% of the nation's total electricity in 2015 which was 45.71% of the total renewable power in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The United States is the <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity#World_hydroelectric_capacity" title="Hydroelectricity">fourth largest producer</a> of hydroelectricity in the world after China, Canada and Brazil. The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Coulee_Dam" title="Grand Coulee Dam">Grand Coulee Dam</a> is the 5th <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_hydroelectric_power_stations" title="List of largest hydroelectric power stations">largest hydroelectric power station</a> in the world.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">U.S. wind power</a> installed capacity now exceeds 72,000 MW and supplies 4.1% of the nation's electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AWEA_2012Q4_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AWEA_2012Q4-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Texas" title="Wind power in Texas">Texas</a> is firmly established as the leader in wind power development, followed by Iowa and California.<sup id="cite_ref-AWE_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AWE-10">[10]</a></sup> Since the U.S. pioneered the technology in 1981 with <a href="/wiki/The_Solar_Project" title="The Solar Project">Solar One</a>, several <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar thermal power">solar thermal power</a> stations have also been built. The largest of these solar thermal power stations are the <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">Ivanpah Solar Power Facility</a> (392 MW), southwest of Las Vegas, and the <a href="/wiki/SEGS" class="mw-redirect" title="SEGS">SEGS</a> group of plants in the Mojave Desert, with a total generating capacity of 354 MW.<sup id="cite_ref-segs_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segs-11">[11]</a></sup> Large <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic power plant">photovoltaic power plants</a> in the USA include <a href="/wiki/Solar_Star" title="Solar Star">Solar Star</a> (579 MW), near <a href="/wiki/Rosamond,_California" title="Rosamond, California">Rosamond, California</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Desert_Sunlight_Solar_Farm" title="Desert Sunlight Solar Farm">Desert Sunlight Solar Farm</a>, a 550 MW <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">Riverside County, California</a><sup id="cite_ref-rew11_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rew11-12">[12]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a>, a 550 MW <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> power plant, in <a href="/wiki/San_Luis_Obispo_County,_California" title="San Luis Obispo County, California">San Luis Obispo County, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-re11_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-re11-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Geysers" title="The Geysers">The Geysers</a> in Northern California is the largest complex of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a> production in the world.</p>
<p>The development of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> and energy efficiency marks "a new era of energy exploration" in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, according to President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/Joint_address_to_the_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Joint address to the Congress">joint address to the Congress</a> on February 24, 2009, President Obama called for doubling renewable energy within the next three years.<sup id="cite_ref-joint2009_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-joint2009-15">[15]</a></sup> In his 2012 <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">State of the Union</a> address, President Barack Obama restated his commitment to renewable energy and mentioned the long-standing Interior Department commitment to permit 10,000 MW of renewable energy projects on public land in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindsay_Morris_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindsay_Morris-16">[16]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationale_for_renewables">Rationale for renewables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Rationale for renewables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Renewable energy technologies encompass a broad, diverse array of technologies, including <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">solar photovoltaics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar thermal power">solar thermal power</a> plants and heating/cooling systems, <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a> plants, and <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">ocean power systems</a> and the use of <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>.</p>
<p>The report <i><a href="/wiki/Outlook_On_Renewable_Energy_In_America" title="Outlook On Renewable Energy In America">Outlook On Renewable Energy In America</a></i> explains that America needs renewable energy, for many reasons:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>America needs energy that is secure, reliable, improves public health, protects the environment, addresses climate change, creates jobs, and provides technological leadership. America needs renewable energy. If renewable energy is to be developed to its full potential, America will need coordinated, sustained federal and state policies that expand renewable energy markets; promote and deploy new technology; and provide appropriate opportunities to encourage renewable energy use in all critical energy market sectors: wholesale and distributed electricity generation, thermal energy applications, and transportation.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Another benefit of some renewable energy technologies, like wind and solar photovoltaics (PV) is that they require little or no water to generate electricity whereas thermoelectric (fossil fuel based) power plants require vast amounts of water for operation.</p>
<p>In 2009, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_inauguration" title="United States presidential inauguration">inaugural address</a> called for the expanded use of renewable energy to meet the twin challenges of <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. Those were the first references ever to the nation's energy use, to renewable resources, and to climate change in an inauguration speech of a U.S. president. President Obama looked to the near future, saying that as a nation, the United States will "harness the sun and the winds and the soil to fuel our cars and run our factories."<sup id="cite_ref-apps1.eere.energy.gov_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apps1.eere.energy.gov-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The president's <i><a href="/wiki/New_Energy_For_America" class="mw-redirect" title="New Energy For America">New Energy For America</a></i> plan calls for a federal investment of $150 billion over the next decade to catalyze private efforts to build a clean energy future. Specifically, the plan calls for renewable energy to supply 10% of the nation's electricity by 2012, rising to 25% by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-apps1.eere.energy.gov_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apps1.eere.energy.gov-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his <a href="/wiki/Joint_address_to_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Joint address to Congress">joint address to Congress</a> in 2009, Obama stated that: "We know the country that harnesses the power of clean, renewable energy will lead the 21st. century....Thanks to our <a href="/wiki/Obama_recovery_plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Obama recovery plan">recovery plan</a>, we will double this nation’s supply of renewable energy in the next three years... It is time for America to lead again".<sup id="cite_ref-joint2009_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-joint2009-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Clean_Edge" title="Clean Edge">Clean Edge</a>, there's little doubt that the future of energy will be cleaner. The transition from carbon-intensive energy sources like wood, coal, and oil to natural gas and renewables, is well underway. For much of the developed world, and for developing nations, the "future looks increasingly like it will be built off of a mix of energy efficiency, renewables, the electrification of transport, and lower carbon fuels like natural gas".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 survey conducted by Applied Materials shows that two-thirds of Americans believe solar technology should play a greater role in meeting the country's energy needs. In addition, "three-quarters of Americans feel that increasing renewable energy and decreasing U.S. dependence on foreign oil are the country's top energy priorities". According to the survey, "67 percent of Americans would be willing to pay more for their monthly utility bill if their utility company increased its use of renewable energy".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2010 <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Council_on_Global_Affairs" title="Chicago Council on Global Affairs">Chicago Council on Global Affairs</a> public opinion survey, an overwhelming 91 percent believed "investing in renewable energy" is important for the United States to remain economically competitive with other countries, with 62 percent considering this very important. The same poll found strong support for tax incentives to encourage development of renewable energy sources specifically as a way to reduce foreign energy imports. Eight in ten (80 percent) favored tax incentives, 47 percent strongly, and only 17 percent were opposed.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2011, new evidence has emerged that there are considerable risks associated with traditional energy sources, and that major changes to the mix of energy technologies is needed:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Several mining tragedies globally have underscored the human toll of the coal supply chain. New EPA initiatives targeting air toxics, coal ash, and effluent releases highlight the environmental impacts of coal and the cost of addressing them with control technologies. The use of fracking in natural gas exploration is coming under scrutiny, with evidence of groundwater contamination and greenhouse gas emissions. Concerns are increasing about the vast amounts of water used at coal-fired and nuclear power plants, particularly in regions of the country facing water shortages. Events at the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear plant</a> have renewed doubts about the ability to operate large numbers of nuclear plants safely over the long term. Further, cost estimates for “next generation” nuclear units continue to climb, and lenders are unwilling to finance these plants without taxpayer guarantees.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_trends">Current trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Current trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USRenewableElectricity.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/USRenewableElectricity.jpg/220px-USRenewableElectricity.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/USRenewableElectricity.jpg/330px-USRenewableElectricity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/USRenewableElectricity.jpg/440px-USRenewableElectricity.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3096" data-file-height="2046" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USRenewableElectricity.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Percentage of electricity in the US generated from renewable sources 1950-2012; hydropower in blue and other renewable sources in red.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Renewable energy in the United States accounted for 13.44 percent of the domestically produced electricity in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-epm_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epm-23">[23]</a></sup> California is a leading state and around 29 percent of California's electricity comes from RPS eligible renewable sources (including hydropower).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United States has some of the best renewable energy resources in the world, which have the potential to meet a rising and significant share of the nation's energy demand. A quarter of the U.S. land area has winds strong enough to generate electricity at the same price as natural gas and coal.<sup id="cite_ref-Cost_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cost-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of the new technologies that harness renewables — including wind, solar, geothermal, and biofuels — are, or soon will be, economically competitive with the fossil fuels that meet 85 percent of U.S. energy needs. Dynamic growth rates are driving down costs and spurring rapid advances in technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Cost_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cost-25">[25]</a></sup> Energy technologies also receive government subsidies. In 2013, federal government energy-specific subsidies and supports for renewables, fossil fuels, and nuclear were $15.043 billion, $3.431 billion and $1.66 billion respectively. The subsidies and supports specific to electricity production amount to $11.678 billion, $1.591 billion and $1.66 billion respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-EIA2013_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA2013-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>All but a few U.S. states now have incentives in place to promote renewable energy, while more than a dozen have enacted new renewable energy laws in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-Cost_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cost-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Renewable energy suffered a political setback in the United States in September 2011 with the bankruptcy of <a href="/wiki/Solyndra" title="Solyndra">Solyndra</a>, a company that had received a $535 million federal loan guarantee.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>

<p>The United States used about 4,000 billion kWh/year of electricity in 2012, and about 98 quadrillion BTU/year (30,000 billion kWh). Efficiency improvements are expected to reduce usage to 15,000 billion kWh by 2050. The United States has the potential of installing 11 million MW of onshore wind power and 4 million MW of offshore wind power, capable of generating over 47,000 billion kWh. The U.S. has the potential of installing 10 to 20 million MW of concentrated solar power in the southwest, capable of generating over 10,000 billion kWh.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>

<p>will result in a total of 627.7 Giga Kilowatt hours (G kwh) of renewable electric energy in 2019. This would be up 89.4 G kwh (+16.7%) from 2014.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hydroelectricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoovernewbridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Hoovernewbridge.jpg/220px-Hoovernewbridge.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Hoovernewbridge.jpg/330px-Hoovernewbridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Hoovernewbridge.jpg/440px-Hoovernewbridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoovernewbridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Dam" title="Hoover Dam">Hoover Dam</a> when completed in 1936 was both the world's largest electric-power generating station and the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> structure.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power_in_the_United_States" title="Hydroelectric power in the United States">Hydroelectric power in the United States</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroelectric power">Hydroelectric power</a> is currently the largest producer of renewable power in the U.S. It produced around 6.14% of the nation's total electricity in 2015 which was 45.71% of the total renewable power in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-34">[34]</a></sup> The United States is the <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity#World_hydroelectric_capacity" title="Hydroelectricity">fourth largest producer</a> of hydroelectricity in the world after China, Canada and Brazil. The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Coulee_Dam" title="Grand Coulee Dam">Grand Coulee Dam</a> is the 5th <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_hydroelectric_power_stations" title="List of largest hydroelectric power stations">largest hydroelectric power station</a> in the world and another six U.S. hydro plants are among the 50 largest in the world. The amount of hydroelectric power generated is strongly affected by changes in precipitation and surface runoff.</p>
<p>Hydroelectric plants exist in at least 34 US states. Hydroelectricity projects such as <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Dam" title="Hoover Dam">Hoover Dam</a>, Grand Coulee Dam, and the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley_Authority" title="Tennessee Valley Authority">Tennessee Valley Authority</a> have become iconic large construction projects.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_power">Wind power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Wind power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">Wind power in the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG/220px-Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG/330px-Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG/440px-Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roscoe_Wind_Farm_at_Sunrise.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 781 MW <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Wind_Farm" title="Roscoe Wind Farm">Roscoe Wind Farm</a> in Texas, at sunrise.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Windmills_south_of_Dumas,_TX_IMG_0570.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Windmills_south_of_Dumas%2C_TX_IMG_0570.JPG/220px-Windmills_south_of_Dumas%2C_TX_IMG_0570.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Windmills_south_of_Dumas%2C_TX_IMG_0570.JPG/330px-Windmills_south_of_Dumas%2C_TX_IMG_0570.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Windmills_south_of_Dumas%2C_TX_IMG_0570.JPG/440px-Windmills_south_of_Dumas%2C_TX_IMG_0570.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Windmills_south_of_Dumas,_TX_IMG_0570.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Landowners typically receive $3,000 to $5,000 per year in rental income from each wind turbine, while farmers continue to grow crops or graze cattle up to the foot of the turbines.<sup id="cite_ref-nine_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nine-38">[38]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>US wind energy capacity nearly tripled from 2008 to 2015. Wind supplied nearly 5% of total US electricity generation in 2015. There was ca 90,000 U.S. wind operations jobs in 2015. Wind and solar accounted for two-thirds of new energy installations in the US in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">U.S. wind power</a> installed capacity now exceeds 72 GW.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-32">[32]</a></sup> This capacity is exceeded only by <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_China" title="Wind power in China">China</a>. The 1,320MW <a href="/wiki/Alta_Wind_Energy_Center" title="Alta Wind Energy Center">Alta Wind Energy Center</a> is the largest wind farm in the world. <a href="/wiki/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm" title="Shepherds Flat Wind Farm">Shepherds Flat Wind Farm</a> in Oregon is the second largest wind farm in the world, completed in 2012, with the nameplate capacity of 845 MW.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. wind industry generates tens of thousands of jobs and billions of dollars of economic activity.<sup id="cite_ref-usdept_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usdept-41">[41]</a></sup> Wind projects boost local tax bases, and revitalize the economy of rural communities by providing a steady income stream to farmers with wind turbines on their land.<sup id="cite_ref-nine_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nine-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GE_Energy" title="GE Energy">GE Energy</a> is the largest domestic <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbine</a> manufacturer.<sup id="cite_ref-nine_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nine-38">[38]</a></sup> In 2013 wind power received $5.936 billion in federal funding, which is 37% of all federal funding for electrity generation.<sup id="cite_ref-EIA2013_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA2013-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012 there were 8,900 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">MW</a> under construction in nearly 100 projects.<sup id="cite_ref-AWEA_2012Q1_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AWEA_2012Q1-42">[42]</a></sup> The United States has the potential of installing 10 million MW of onshore wind power and 4 million MW of offshore wind.<sup id="cite_ref-aweastats_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aweastats-43">[43]</a></sup> The U.S. Department of Energy’s report <i>20% Wind Energy by 2030</i> envisioned that wind power could supply 20% of all U.S. electricity, which included a contribution of 4% from <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">offshore wind power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usdept_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usdept-41">[41]</a></sup> Additional transmission lines will need to be added, to bring power from windy states to the rest of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In August 2011, a coalition of 24 governors asked the Obama administration to provide a more favorable business climate for the development of wind power.<sup id="cite_ref-2011rew_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011rew-45">[45]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_Power">Solar Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Solar Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Solar power will be discussed in three distinct varieties . Electric energy is generated by two different methods -Thermal and Photovoltaic. Application is either utility scale for electric grid power generation or much smaller distributed systems which may or may not be integrated into the electric grid. Utility grade systems have well documented generation but distributed systems contributions to user electric power needs are not measured or controlled. Therefore, quantitative evaluation of distributive solar to the overall US electric power environment has been lacking. Recently, the Energy Information Administration.<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-54">[54]</a></sup> has begun estimating that contribution.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png/220px-2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png/330px-2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png/440px-2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="289" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2006-2015_Solar_Electric_Energy_with_2014-2015_Estimated_Distributed_Solar.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
2006-2015 Solar Electric Energy with 2014-2015 Estimated Distributed Solar<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-54">[54]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2015, utility scale solar<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-36">[36]</a></sup> contributed 26.47 G kWh (billion kWh) to the grid with 23.233 G kWh from photovoltaics and 3.24 G kWh from thermal systems. In 2014 and 2015, EIA estimated that distributed solar&gt;<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-54">[54]</a></sup> generated 9.536 G kWh and 12.141 G kWh respectively.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png/220px-2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png/330px-2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png/440px-2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png 2x" data-file-width="507" data-file-height="348" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2015_Major_Solar_Farms.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png/220px-2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png/330px-2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png/440px-2015_Solar_projects_Capacity_Factors.png 2x" data-file-width="472" data-file-height="324" /></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_thermal_power_utility_scale">Solar thermal power utility scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Solar thermal power utility scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_plants_in_the_Mojave_Desert" title="Solar power plants in the Mojave Desert">Solar power plants in the Mojave Desert</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%281%29.jpg/220px-Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%281%29.jpg/330px-Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%281%29.jpg/440px-Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1124" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Looking north towards the <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">Ivanpah Solar Power Facility</a>'s eastern boiler tower from <a href="/wiki/Interstate_15" title="Interstate 15">Interstate 15</a> in California.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prior to 2012, in six southwestern states (Arizona, California, Colorado, Nevada, New Mexico, and Utah) the US Bureau of Land Management owned nearly 98 million acres (an area larger than the state of Montana) that was open to proposals for solar power installations. To streamline consideration of applications, the BLM produced a Programmatic Environmental Impact Statement (PEIS). By the subsequent Record of Decision in October 2012, the BLM withdrew 78 percent of its land from possible solar development, leaving 19 million acres still open to applications for solar installations, an area nearly as large as South Carolina. Of the area left open to solar proposals, the BLM has identified 285 thousand acres in 17 highly favorable areas it calls Solar Energy Zones.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-anl.gov_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anl.gov-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-anl.gov_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anl.gov-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems">Solar Energy Generating Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Solar Energy Generating Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">Solar Energy Generating Systems</a> (SEGS) is the name given to nine <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar power plants">solar power plants</a> in the Mojave Desert. SEGS I-VII are located at Kramer Junction, and SEGS VIII and IX are at Harper Lake and Barstow respectively. The SEGS power plants were commissioned between 1984 and 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-segs_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segs-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The installation uses <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">parabolic trough</a> <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar thermal">solar thermal</a> technology along with <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> to generate electricity. The facility has a total of 400,000 mirrors and covers 1,000 acres (4&#160;km²). The plants have a total generating capacity of 354 MW.<sup id="cite_ref-segs_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segs-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nevada_Solar_One">Nevada Solar One</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nevada Solar One">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nevada_Solar_One" title="Nevada Solar One">Nevada Solar One</a> generates 64MW of power and in <a href="/wiki/Boulder_City,_Nevada" title="Boulder City, Nevada">Boulder City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, and was built by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy (DOE)</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), and Solargenix Energy. Nevada Solar One started producing electricity in June 2007.</p>
<p>Nevada Solar One uses <a href="/wiki/Trough_concentrator" class="mw-redirect" title="Trough concentrator">parabolic troughs</a> as <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">thermal solar concentrators</a>, heating tubes of liquid which act as solar receivers. These solar receivers are specially coated tubes made of glass and steel. About 19,300 of these 4 metre long tubes are used in the newly built power plant. Nevada Solar One also uses a technology that collects extra heat by putting it into phase-changing molten salts. This energy can then be drawn on at night.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Solar thermal power plants designed for solar-only generation are well matched to summer noon peak loads in prosperous areas with significant cooling demands, such as the south-western United States. Using thermal energy storage systems, solar thermal operating periods can even be extended to meet base-load needs.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility">Ivanpah Solar Power Facility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">Ivanpah Solar Power Facility</a> is a 392 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatt</a> (MW) solar power facility which is located in south-eastern California.<sup id="cite_ref-mufson_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mufson-60">[60]</a></sup> The facility formally opened on February 13, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-AP_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solana_Generating_Station" title="Solana Generating Station">Solana Generating Station</a> is a 280 MW <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> plant which is near <a href="/wiki/Gila_Bend,_Arizona" title="Gila Bend, Arizona">Gila Bend</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, about 70 miles (110&#160;km) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a>. The 250MW <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Solar_Project" title="Mojave Solar Project">Mojave Solar Project</a> is located near <a href="/wiki/Barstow,_California" title="Barstow, California">Barstow, California</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Crescent_Dunes_Solar_Energy_Project" title="Crescent Dunes Solar Energy Project">Crescent Dunes Solar Energy Project</a> is a 110&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatt</a> (MW) <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar thermal power">solar thermal power</a> project near <a href="/wiki/Tonopah,_Nevada" title="Tonopah, Nevada">Tonopah</a>, about 190 miles (310&#160;km) northwest of <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas,_Nevada" class="mw-redirect" title="Las Vegas, Nevada">Las Vegas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-doe-2011-09-28_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doe-2011-09-28-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use_issues">Land use issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Land use issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A 2013 study by the US National Renewable Energy Laboratory concluded that utility-scale solar power plants directly disturb an average of 2.7 to 2.9 acres per gigawatt-hour/year, and use from 3.5 to 3.8 acres per gW-hr/year for the entire sites. According to a 2009 study, this intensity of land use is less than that of the average US power plant using surface-mined coal.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Some of the land in the eastern portion of the Mojave Desert is to be preserved, but the solar industry is more interested in areas of the western desert, "where the sun burns hotter and there is easier access to transmission lines".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Generation">Generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_photovoltaic_power_utility_scale">Solar photovoltaic power utility scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Solar photovoltaic power utility scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg/220px-Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg/330px-Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg/440px-Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1633" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nellis_AFB_Solar_panels.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nellis_Solar_Power_Plant" title="Nellis Solar Power Plant">Nellis Solar Power Plant</a> at Nellis Air Force Base. These panels track the sun in one axis.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">Solar power in the United States</a></div>
<p>At the end of 2014, the U.S. had 18.3 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatts</a> (GW) of installed photovoltaic capacity with an additional 1.7 GW of <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The U.S. pioneered solar farms and many key developments in photovoltaics came out of U.S. research.</p>
<p>The U.S. has some of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">largest solar farms</a> in the world. <a href="/wiki/Solar_Star" title="Solar Star">Solar Star</a> is a 579 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatt</a> (MW<a href="/wiki/Watt-peak_AC" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-peak AC"><sub>AC</sub></a>) farm near <a href="/wiki/Rosamond,_California" title="Rosamond, California">Rosamond, California</a>. Completed in June 2015, it uses 1.7 million solar panels, spread over 13 square kilometers (3,200 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Desert_Sunlight_Solar_Farm" title="Desert Sunlight Solar Farm">Desert Sunlight Solar Farm</a> is a 550 MW <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">Riverside County, California</a>, that uses thin-film solar <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> modules made by <a href="/wiki/First_Solar" title="First Solar">First Solar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rew11_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rew11-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a> is a 550 MW photovoltaic power plant, in <a href="/wiki/San_Luis_Obispo_County,_California" title="San Luis Obispo County, California">San Luis Obispo County, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-re11_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-re11-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Blythe_Solar_Power_Project" class="mw-redirect" title="Blythe Solar Power Project">Blythe Solar Power Project</a> is a 485 MW photovoltaic station planned for <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">Riverside County, California</a>.</p>
<p>Many schools and businesses have <a href="/wiki/Building-integrated_photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Building-integrated photovoltaic">building-integrated photovoltaic solar</a> panels on their roof. Most of these are grid connected and use <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a> laws to allow use of electricity in the evening that was generated during the daytime. New Jersey leads the nation with the least restrictive net metering law, while California leads in total number of homes which have solar panels installed. Many were installed because of the million solar roof initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> California decided that it is not moving forward fast enough on photovoltaic generation and in 2008 enacted a <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_Tariff#California" class="mw-redirect" title="Feed-in Tariff">feed-in tariff</a>. Washington state has a feed-in tariff of 15 ¢/kWh which increases to 54 ¢/kWh if components are manufactured in the state.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> By 2015, California, Hawaii, Arizona and some other states were lowering payments to <a href="/wiki/Distributed_solar" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed solar">distributed solar</a> owners and instituting new fees for grid usage. Tesla and a handful of other companies were promoting household grid-tied batteries while some electric companies were investing in utility-scale <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">grid energy storage</a> including very large batteries.</p>
<p>The contribution to the US electric grid over the last ten years of solar photovoltaic electric power to the renewable power generation and to the total US power generation is shown below along with the yearly profile of the photovoltaic power generation for 2014 and 2015. This shows the typical variations over the months of the year due to sunlight availability. Before 2008, most solar-generated electric energy was from thermal systems. By 2011, photovoltaics had overtaken thermal.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estimated_distributed_solar_capacity">Estimated distributed solar capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Estimated distributed solar capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg/220px-Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg/330px-Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg/440px-Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photovoltaic solar panels on a house roof</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning with the 2014 data year, Energy Information Administration will estimate distributed solar photovoltaic generation and distributed solar photovoltaic capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-53">[53]</a></sup> These non-utility scale estimates project that, in 2014 the USA, generated a further 9.536 GWh and in 2015 an additional 12.141 GWh of solar electricity from such distributed PV systems.<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png/220px-Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png/330px-Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png/440px-Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="289" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Top_Five_States_for_Distributed_Solar.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Top Five States for Distributed Solar<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-54">[54]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png/220px-Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png/330px-Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png/440px-Estimated_Distributed_Solar_by_States_with_less_than_600_M_kWh.png 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="726" /></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geothermal_power">Geothermal power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Geothermal power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy_in_the_United_States" title="Geothermal energy in the United States">Geothermal energy in the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg/300px-2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg" width="300" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg/450px-2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg/600px-2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3300" data-file-height="2550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2013_02_28_Geothermal_Capacity-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Existing and planned US geothermal power generation, as of February 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The USA is the world leader in online capacity and the generation of electricity from geothermal energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Geothermal_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geothermal-72">[72]</a></sup> According to 2014 state energy data, geothermal energy provided approximately 16 billion kilowatt hours (kWh) of electricity—0.31% of the electricity consumed in the U.S. As of May 2007, geothermal electric power was generated in five U.S. states: Alaska, California, Hawaii, Nevada, and Utah. According to the <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_Energy_Association" title="Geothermal Energy Association">Geothermal Energy Association</a>'s recent report, there were 75 new geothermal power projects underway in 12 states as of May 2007 . This is an increase of 14 projects in an additional three states compared to a survey completed in November 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Geothermal_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geothermal-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most significant catalyst behind new industry activity is the <i><a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">Energy Policy Act of 2005</a></i>. This Act made new geothermal plants eligible for the full federal production tax credit, previously available only to wind power projects. It also authorized and directed increased funding for research by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a>, and gave the Bureau of Land Management new legal guidance and secure funding to address its backlog of geothermal leases and permits.<sup id="cite_ref-Geothermal_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geothermal-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Installed geothermal capacity in megawatts (MW) by state as of February 2013:<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_water_heating">Solar water heating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Solar water heating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The U.S. Department of Energy stated (in 2006) that more than 1.5 million homes and businesses were currently using <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">solar water heating</a> in the United States, representing a capacity of over 1,000 megawatts (MW) of thermal energy generation. It predicted that another 400 MW was likely to be installed over the next 3–5 years.</p>
<p>Assuming that 40 percent of existing homes in the United States have adequate access to sunlight, 29 million solar water heaters could be installed.<sup id="cite_ref-SWH_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWH-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Solar water heaters can operate in any climate. Performance varies depending on how much solar energy is available at the site, as well as how cold the water coming into the system is. The colder the water, the more efficiently the system operates.<sup id="cite_ref-SWH_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWH-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Solar water heaters reduce the need for conventional water heating by about two-thirds and pay for their installation within 4 to 8 years with electricity or natural gas savings. Compared to those with electric water heaters, Florida homeowners with solar water heaters save 50 to 85 percent on their water heating bills, according to the Florida Solar Energy Center.<sup id="cite_ref-SWH_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWH-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomass">Biomass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biomass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a></div>
<p>In 2015, biomass generated 64.19 million megawatt-hours, 1.57% of total US generated electricity. It was the largest source of total renewable energy in the US, and the third-largest renewable source of electrical power in the US, after hydropower and wind.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biomass is biological material derived from living, or recently living organisms, such as plants and trees and utilizes wastes or plant matter specifically grown to generate electricity or produce heat. The main advantage of using grown fuels, as opposed to fossil fuels such as coal, natural gas and oil, is that while they are growing they absorb the near-equivalent in carbon dioxide (an important greenhouse gas) to that which is later released in their burning. Although there is some debate over the net carbon neutrality and near term affects of using the biomass for energy a key difference is the relatively short carbon recycle period of grown biomass (several years or decades) as opposed to the millions of years it took to turn carbon into fossil fuels. With proper conservation and growing techniques biomass can be an important renewable energy source.</p>
<p>Biomass can be utilized for all three major energy needs: electricity, heating/cooling and transportation fuels. However, each usage is distinctly different from the others, especially regarding efficiency - the percentage of energy utilized from the biomass source. Whereas pellets for heating can be up to 90% efficient, bio-electricity plants are comparable to coal power plants, with around 30% efficiency, and biofuels from crops or algae even less.</p>
<p>Biomass electric generation data combines two basic categories: 1) Wood and wood derived fuels including wood/wood waste solids (including paper pellets, railroad ties, utility poles, wood chiips, bark and wood waste solids), wood waste liquids (red liquor, sludge wood, spent sulfite liquor, and other wood based liquids), and black liquor. and, 2) Other biomass fuels include municipal solid waste, landfill gas, sludge waste agricultural byproducts, other biomass solids, other biomass liquids, and other biomass gases (including digester gases, methane, and other biomass gases). The contribution from these two categories over the last ten years of biomass electric power to the renewable power generation and to the total US power generation is shown below along with the yearly profile of the electric power generation for 2014 and 2015. This shows the typical variations over the months of the year due to fuel availability and needs. |</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png/220px-2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png/330px-2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png/440px-2015_Wood_Electric_Energy_Generation_Profile.png 2x" data-file-width="507" data-file-height="338" /></a>
