<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuels">Biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EthanolPetrol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/EthanolPetrol.jpg/220px-EthanolPetrol.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/EthanolPetrol.jpg/330px-EthanolPetrol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/EthanolPetrol.jpg/440px-EthanolPetrol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="574" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EthanolPetrol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Information on pump, California.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biofuel_in_the_United_States" title="Biofuel in the United States">Biofuel in the United States</a></div>
<p>Most cars on the road today in the U.S. can run on blends of up to 10% <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol</a>, and motor vehicle manufacturers already produce vehicles designed to run on much higher ethanol blends. <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/DaimlerChrysler" class="mw-redirect" title="DaimlerChrysler">DaimlerChrysler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors Corporation">GM</a> are among the automobile companies that sell “flexible-fuel” cars, trucks, and minivans that can use gasoline and ethanol blends ranging from pure gasoline up to 85% ethanol (E85). By mid-2006, there were approximately six million E85-compatible vehicles on U.S. roads.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The challenge is to expand the market for biofuels beyond the farm states where they have been most popular to date.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Flex-fuel vehicles are assisting in this transition because they allow drivers to choose different fuels based on price and availability. The <i><a href="/wiki/Energy_Independence_and_Security_Act_of_2007" title="Energy Independence and Security Act of 2007">Energy Independence and Security Act of 2007</a></i>, which calls for 15.2&#160;billion US gallons (58,000,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of biofuels to be used annually by 2012, will also help to expand the market.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The expanding ethanol and <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> industries are providing jobs in plant construction, operations, and maintenance, mostly in rural communities. According to the Renewable Fuels Association, the ethanol industry created almost 154,000 U.S. jobs in 2005 alone, boosting household income by $5.7 billion. It also contributed about $3.5 billion in tax revenues at the local, state, and federal levels.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-77">[77]</a></sup> On the other hand, in 2010, the biofuel industry received $6644 million in federal government support.<sup id="cite_ref-EIA2010_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA2010-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_power">Wave power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Wave power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wave_Power_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Wave Power in the United States">Wave Power in the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Optbuoy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Optbuoy.jpg/220px-Optbuoy.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Optbuoy.jpg/330px-Optbuoy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Optbuoy.jpg/440px-Optbuoy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="581" data-file-height="757" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Optbuoy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
PB150 PowerBuoy with peak-rated power output of 150 kW.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wave power in the United States is under development in several locations off the east and west coasts as well as Hawaii. It has moved beyond the research phase and is producing reliable energy. Its use to-date has been for situations where other forms of energy production are not economically viable and as such, the power output is currently modest. But major installations are planned to come on-line within the next few years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_research">Renewable energy research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Renewable energy research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are numerous organizations within the academic, federal, and commercial sectors conducting large scale advanced research in the field of renewable energy. This research spans several areas of focus across the renewable energy spectrum. Most of the research is targeted at improving efficiency and increasing overall energy yields.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Multiple federally supported research organizations have focused on renewable energy in recent years. Two of the most prominent of these labs are <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), both of which are funded by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> and supported by various corporate partners.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Sandia has a total budget of $2.4 billion<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> while NREL has a budget of $375 million.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), have heavily funded solar research programs. <a href="/wiki/British_Petroleum" class="mw-redirect" title="British Petroleum">British Petroleum</a> was also heavily invested in solar research programs until 2008 when the company began scaling back its solar operations. The company finally shut down its forty-year-old solar business after executives decided solar power production is not economically competitive.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The NREL solar program has a budget of around $75 million<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and develops research projects in the areas of <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> (PV) technology, solar thermal energy, and solar radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The budget for Sandia’s solar division is unknown, however it accounts for a significant percentage of the laboratory’s $2.4 billion budget.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Several academic programs have focused on solar research in recent years. The Solar Energy Research Center (SERC) at <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina" title="University of North Carolina">University of North Carolina</a> (UNC) has the sole purpose of developing cost effective solar technology. In 2008, researchers at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT) developed a method to store solar energy by using it to produce hydrogen fuel from water.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Such research is targeted at addressing the obstacle that solar development faces of storing energy for use during nighttime hours when the sun is not shining. In February 2012, North Carolina-based Semprius Inc., a solar development company backed by German corporation <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, announced that they had developed the world’s most efficient solar panel. The company claims that the prototype converts 33.9% of the sunlight that hits it to electricity, more than double the previous high-end conversion rate.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wind energy research dates back several decades to the 1970s when <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> developed an analytical model to predict wind turbine power generation during high winds.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Today, both <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> have programs dedicated to wind research. Sandia’s laboratory focuses on the advancement of materials, aerodynamics, and sensors.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The NREL wind projects are centered on improving wind plant power production, reducing their capital costs, and making wind energy more cost effective overall.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The Field Laboratory for Optimized Wind Energy (FLOWE) at <a href="/wiki/Caltech" class="mw-redirect" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> was established to research renewable approaches to wind energy farming technology practices that have the potential to reduce the cost, size, and environmental impact of wind energy production.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the primary source of biofuels in North America, many organizations are conducting research in the area of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> production. On the Federal level, the <a href="/wiki/USDA" class="mw-redirect" title="USDA">USDA</a> conducts a large amount of research regarding ethanol production in the United States. Much of this research is targeted toward the effect of ethanol production on domestic food markets.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> has conducted various ethanol research projects, mainly in the area of <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">Cellulosic ethanol</a> has many benefits over traditional corn based-ethanol. It does not take away or directly conflict with the food supply because it is produced from wood, grasses, or non-edible parts of plants.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Moreover, some studies have shown cellulosic ethanol to be more cost effective and economically sustainable than corn-based ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> conducts in-house cellulosic ethanol research<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_BioEnergy_Institute" title="Joint BioEnergy Institute">Joint BioEnergy Institute</a> (JBEI), a research institute founded by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> with the goal of developing cellulosic biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over $1 billion of federal money has been spent on the research and development of hydrogen fuel in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Both the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> have departments dedicated to hydrogen research.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy_and_promotion">Policy and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Policy and promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In his January 24, 2012, <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">State of the Union</a> address, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> restated his commitment to renewable energy, stating that he “will not walk away from the promise of clean energy.” Obama called for a commitment by the <a href="/wiki/Defence_minister" title="Defence minister">Defense Department</a> to purchase 1,000 MW of renewable energy. He also mentioned the long-standing <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">Interior Department</a> commitment to permit 10,000 MW of renewable energy projects on public land in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindsay_Morris_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindsay_Morris-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Recovery_and_Reinvestment_Act_of_2009" title="American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009">American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009</a> included more than $70 billion in direct spending and tax credits for <a href="/wiki/Clean_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Clean energy">clean energy</a> and associated transportation programs. This policy-stimulus combination represents the largest federal commitment in U.S. history for <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, advanced transportation, and <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">energy conservation</a> initiatives. These new initiatives were expected to encourage many more utilities to strengthen their clean energy programs.<sup id="cite_ref-obama_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obama-103">[103]</a></sup> While the Department of Energy has come under criticism for providing loan guarantees to Solyndra,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> its SunShot initiative has funded successful companies such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=EnergySage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="EnergySage (page does not exist)">EnergySage</a><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Zep_Solar" title="Zep Solar">Zep Solar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiatives">Initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SunShot">SunShot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: SunShot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In February 2011, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> (DOE) launched its SunShot initiative, a collaborative national effort to cut the total cost of photovoltaic solar energy systems by 75% by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Reaching this goal would make unsubsidized solar energy cost-competitive with other forms of electricity and get <a href="/wiki/Grid_parity" title="Grid parity">grid parity</a> .<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The SunShot initiative included a crowdsourced innovation program run in partnership with <a href="/wiki/TopCoder" title="TopCoder">Topcoder</a>, during which 17 different solar energy application solutions were developed in 60 days.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In 2011, the price was $4/W, and the SunShot goal of $1/W by 2020 was reached in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_Powering_America">Wind Powering America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Wind Powering America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_Powering_America_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind Powering America Initiative">Wind Powering America</a> (WPA) is another DOE initiative that seeks to increase the use of wind energy. WPA collaborates with state and regional stakeholders, including farmers, ranchers, Native Americans, rural electric cooperatives, consumer-owned utilities and schools.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>WPA has focused on states with strong potential for wind energy generation but with few operational projects. WPA provides information about the challenges, benefits, and impacts of wind technology implementation.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_America_Initiative">Solar America Initiative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Solar America Initiative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">Solar America Initiative</a> (SAI)<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> is a part of the Federal <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Energy_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="Advanced Energy Initiative">Advanced Energy Initiative</a> to accelerate the development of advanced photovoltaic materials with the goal of making it cost-competitive with other forms of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable electricity">renewable electricity</a> by 2015.</p>
<p>The DOE Solar Energy Technology Program (SETP) intended to achieve the goals of the SAI through partnerships and strategic alliances by focusing primarily on four areas:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Market_transformation" title="Market transformation">Market Transformation</a> — activities that address marketplace barriers</li>
<li>Device and Process Proof of Concept — R&amp;D activities that address novel devices or processes with significant performance or cost advantages</li>
<li>Component Prototype and Pilot-Scale Production — R&amp;D activities emphasizing development of prototype photovoltaic (PV) components or systems at pilot-scale with demonstrated cost, reliability or performance advantages</li>
<li>System Development and Manufacturing — collaborative R&amp;D activities among industry and university partners</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="California_Solar_Initiative">California Solar Initiative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: California Solar Initiative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/California_Solar_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="California Solar Initiative">California Solar Initiative</a></div>
<p>As part of former Governor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a>'s Million Solar Roofs Program, California set a goal to create 3,000 megawatts of new, solar-produced electricity by 2017, with funding of $2.8 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/California_Solar_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="California Solar Initiative">California Solar Initiative</a> offers cash incentives on solar PV systems of up to $2.50 a watt. These incentives, combined with federal tax incentives, can cover up to 50% of the total cost of a solar panel system.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-112">[112]</a></sup> Financial incentives to support renewable energy are available in some other US states.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="EPA_facilities">EPA facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: EPA facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> facilities in the United States use renewable energy for all or part of their supply at the following facilities:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ada, Oklahoma (geothermal heat pump)</li>
<li>Ann Arbor, Michigan (fuel cell)</li>
<li>Chicago, Illinois, Regional Office (photovoltaic array)</li>
<li>Corvallis, Oregon (photovoltaic array)</li>
<li>Edison, New Jersey (solar water heating)</li>
<li>Gulf Breeze, Florida (solar lighting)</li>
<li>Golden, Colorado (wind power and transpired solar collector)</li>
<li>Manchester, Washington (wind power)</li>
<li>Research Triangle Park, North Carolina (photovoltaic solar panels and street lights).<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_Power_Partnership">Green Power Partnership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Green Power Partnership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Green_Power_Partnership" title="Green Power Partnership">Green Power Partnership</a></div>
<p>The EPA named the top 20 partners in its Green Power Partnership that are generating their own renewable energy on-site. Combined, they generate more than 736 million kilowatt-hours of renewable energy on-site each year, enough to power more than 61,000 average U.S. homes.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_portfolio_standards">Renewable portfolio standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Renewable portfolio standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Renewable_Portfolio_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable Portfolio Standard">Renewable Portfolio Standard</a> refers to legislation that creates a market in tradeable renewable or green electricity certificates. Electricity distributors or wholesaler purchasers of electricity are required to source a specified percentage of their electricity (portfolio) from renewable generation sources. Liable entities that fall short of their quota can purchase certificates from accredited suppliers who have generated renewable electricity and obtained and registered certificates to sell on that market.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_to_the_grid">Sales to the grid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sales to the grid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">Energy Policy Act of 2005</a> requires all <a href="/wiki/Public_electric_utility" class="mw-redirect" title="Public electric utility">public electric utilities</a> to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nm_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nm-116">[116]</a></sup> This allows homes and businesses performing <a href="/wiki/Distributed_generation" title="Distributed generation">distributed generation</a> to pay only the net cost of electricity from the grid: electricity used minus electricity produced locally and sent back into the grid. For intermittent renewable energy sources this effectively uses the grid as a <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a> to smooth over lulls and fill in production gaps.</p>
<p>Some jurisdictions go one step further and have instituted <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariff</a>, which allows any power customer to actually make money by producing more renewable energy than is consumed locally.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_effects">Distribution effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Distribution effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From 2006-14, US households received more than $18 billion in federal income <a href="/wiki/Tax_credit" title="Tax credit">tax credits</a> for <a href="/wiki/Weatherization" title="Weatherization">weatherizing</a> their homes, installing solar panels, buying hybrid and electric vehicles, and other "clean energy" investments. These tax expenditures went predominantly to higher-income Americans. The bottom three income quintiles received about 10% of all credits, while the top quintile received about 60%. The most extreme is the program aimed at electric vehicles, where the top income quintile received about 90% of all credits. Market mechanisms have less skewed distributional effects.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_organizations">Renewable energy organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Renewable energy organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Council_on_Renewable_Energy" title="American Council on Renewable Energy">American Council on Renewable Energy</a> (ACORE), is a non-profit organization with headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Washington_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington DC">Washington DC</a>. It was founded in 2001 as a unifying forum for bringing renewable energy into the mainstream of American’s economy and lifestyle. In 2010 ACORE had over 700 member organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In 2007, ACORE published <a href="/wiki/Outlook_On_Renewable_Energy_In_America" title="Outlook On Renewable Energy In America">Outlook On Renewable Energy In America</a>, a two volume report about the future of renewable energy in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> It has been said that this report exposes a "new reality for renewable energy in America."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Environmental_and_Energy_Study_Institute" title="Environmental and Energy Study Institute">Environmental and Energy Study Institute</a> (EESI) is a non-profit <a href="/wiki/Environmental_organization" title="Environmental organization">organization</a> which promotes environmentally <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable</a> societies. Founded in 1984 by a group of <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of the United States">Congressional Members</a>, EESI seeks to be a catalyst that moves society away from environmentally damaging <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> and toward a clean energy future. EESI presents policy solutions that will result in decreased <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>; improvements in public health, <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> and rural economic development opportunities; increased use of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources and improved <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a>.</p>
<p>An important part of the mission of the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL) is the transfer of NREL-developed technologies to renewable energy markets. NREL's Technology Transfer Office supports laboratory scientists and engineers in the successful and practical application of their expertise and the technologies they develop. R&amp;D staff and facilities are recognized and valued by industry, as demonstrated through many collaborative research projects and licensed technologies with public and private partners. NREL's innovative technologies have also been recognized with 39 <a href="/w/index.php?title=R%26D_100_Awards&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="R&amp;D 100 Awards (page does not exist)">R&amp;D 100 Awards</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_Institute" title="Rocky Mountain Institute">Rocky Mountain Institute</a> (RMI) is an organization dedicated to research, publication, consulting, and lecturing in the general field of <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, with a special focus on profitable innovations for energy and resource efficiency. RMI is headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Snowmass,_Colorado" title="Snowmass, Colorado">Snowmass, Colorado</a>, and also maintains offices in <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>. RMI is the publisher of the book <i><a href="/wiki/Winning_the_Oil_Endgame" title="Winning the Oil Endgame">Winning the Oil Endgame</a></i>.</p>
